  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6003af3d-5941-4681-8ad6-31ba8f4444c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer9875197543065921335">Richard Siken’s <i>Crush</i>, selected as the 2004 winner of the Yale Younger Poets prize, is a powerful collection of poems driven by obsession and love. Siken writes with ferocity, and his reader hurtles unstoppably with him. His poetry is confessional, gay, savage, and charged with violent eroticism. In the world of American poetry, Siken's voice is striking. In her introducti</span>
  <span id="freeText9875197543065921335" style="display:none">Richard Siken’s <i>Crush</i>, selected as the 2004 winner of the Yale Younger Poets prize, is a powerful collection of poems driven by obsession and love. Siken writes with ferocity, and his reader hurtles unstoppably with him. His poetry is confessional, gay, savage, and charged with violent eroticism. In the world of American poetry, Siken's voice is striking. In her introduction to the book, competition judge Louise Glück hails the “cumulative, driving, apocalyptic power, [and] purgatorial recklessness” of Siken’s poems. She notes, “Books of this kind dream big. . . . They restore to poetry that sense of crucial moment and crucial utterance which may indeed be the great genius of the form.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="9875197543065921335" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<a href="/questions/525942-i-read-the-poem-you-are-jeff-online-and">I read the poem &#39;You Are Jeff&#39; online and completely fell in love. My question is, is this a book a teen could read? As in, would a parent be okay with their teen reading this?</a>
</div>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/11851382-readtolive"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1347359250i/11851382._UY100_CR31,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/11851382-readtolive">Readtolive</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Well it really depends on your teenager, age and maturity wise. The book is excellent with intense, sometimes graphic descriptions. I would wait if he<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Well it really depends on your teenager, age and maturity wise. The book is excellent with intense, sometimes graphic descriptions. I would wait if he/she is within the 12-15 band [older than that it would be alright but still depends on you].In any case the book is highly recommended, read it first and you can get to decide yourself on it! (it is short too) <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_176344352" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/176344352" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176344352?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176344352" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10013146485051131214">"<em>and the gentleness that comes,<br/>not from the absence of violence, but despite<br/>the abundance of it.</em>"<br/><br/>I read Richard Siken's poem "Wishbone" on the internet. I read it once and closed the page. The next day I was painfully aware I couldn't leave it behind. That my mind kept circling back to his words. So I read it and re-read it again and again. Dozens of times, until I realised it would never be enough, so I ordered his book.<br/><br/>I have never in my life anticipated the arrival of a book more than I did w</span>
  <span id="freeText10013146485051131214" style="display:none">"<em>and the gentleness that comes,<br />not from the absence of violence, but despite<br />the abundance of it.</em>"<br /><br />I read Richard Siken's poem "Wishbone" on the internet. I read it once and closed the page. The next day I was painfully aware I couldn't leave it behind. That my mind kept circling back to his words. So I read it and re-read it again and again. Dozens of times, until I realised it would never be enough, so I ordered his book.<br /><br />I have never in my life anticipated the arrival of a book more than I did with this. My entire body was aching for it. And then it arrived. <br /><br />With a start like this, and with expectations as high as mine, you'd think the book would come up short. That it would somehow not deliver. But it does. It truly fucking does. <br /><br />Siken is beyond talented with words, that much is clear, this entire collection is a work of pure art, something you rarely find these days. Every line is powerful, it's got secrets. Every poem has meaning, and soul and something deeply terrifying about it. <br /><br />I am in love with it. That's the easiest way to put it. My copy is worn out from being opened, read in, then thrown onto the table or put carelessly down as I try to gather myself up from my messy emotional pile on the floor and try to deal with, well... myself. <br /><br />I've read many books, some of them have taught me about the world, about people, about feelings or ideas. This book taught me something monumental about myself. <br /><br />It changed me, and I'm not even kidding or exaggerating. I read it (or devoured it might be more accurate) and suddenly found a side of myself put into words. Words I was never able to find myself, but needed more deeply than I'd realised.<br /><br />I'll never stop reading this book, and that's the great thing with poetry, analyzing, understanding and interpreting and simply feeling it, is a never-ending process. I carry his words with me everywhere, both in the shape of his actual book, but also in who I am. <br /><br />"<em>Tell me how all this, and love too, will ruin us.<br />These, our bodies, possessed by light. <br />Tell me we'll never get used to it.</em>"</span>
  <a data-text-id="10013146485051131214" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17046748966288978125">I felt like I had the wind knocked out of me after I read this. There's a thread of a story here, but it's abstract and shadowed. Almost a ghost of a story. What's left are the raw emotions of the actual experience, which is what great poetry is: distilling the massive events that make up a life until there's nothing left but the urgent parts, the ones that carry the meaning.</span>

<div id="review_281153435" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281153435" />
    <a title="C" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2659381-c"><img alt="C" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491425819p2/2659381.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/281153435?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C" class="user" itemprop="url" name="C" href="/user/show/2659381-c">C</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2659381-c?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer281153435" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer740989726996166993">Ugh why does everyone love this book? Siken, the winner of the 2004 Yale Series, is clearly a capable poet, and there were a few moments in this collection that were beautiful and lucid. Otherwise, though, the poems are so overblown (too many words going in too many directions) and drowning in imagery of bodies, knives, and death. Oh, and SO much cheesy, disembodied dialogue. <br/><br/>The form of the poems in this collection felt like a cop-out: you can only splatter lines across a page so many times bef</span>
  <span id="freeText740989726996166993" style="display:none">Ugh why does everyone love this book? Siken, the winner of the 2004 Yale Series, is clearly a capable poet, and there were a few moments in this collection that were beautiful and lucid. Otherwise, though, the poems are so overblown (too many words going in too many directions) and drowning in imagery of bodies, knives, and death. Oh, and SO much cheesy, disembodied dialogue. <br /><br />The form of the poems in this collection felt like a cop-out: you can only splatter lines across a page so many times before I become suspicious that your form isn't serving the poem, you're just doing it because you can't seem to do anything else successfully. I also thought his endings consistently flopped: almost half the poems end with some form of repetition (either direct or implied). Two of the most obvious examples: <br /><br />"You, the moon. You, the road. You, the little flowers/by the side of the road. You keep singing along to that song I hate. Stop singing." -Road Music<br /><br />"It spins like a wheel inside you: green yellow, green blue,/green beautiful green./It's simple: it isn't over, it's just begun. It's green. It's still green." -Meanwhile<br /><br />I think what really bothers me about the book is the lack of modulation in the narrator's obsessions, mood, and tone. Unless you are a brilliant, brilliant poet, I don't want to read a whole collection of your poems that are set in a forest or come out of a panic or pine endlessly after a lover. This is just one of those books that you can read all the way through and, the next day, not remember more than a handful of images or lines because there is too much junk crowding the beauty out.</span>
  <a data-text-id="740989726996166993" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2462288694" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2462288694" />
    <a title="JV (semi-hiatus)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus"><img alt="JV (semi-hiatus)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559624981p2/20906165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2462288694?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JV (semi-hiatus)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JV (semi-hiatus)" href="/user/show/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus">JV (semi-hiatus)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20906165-jv-semi-hiatus?shelf=gay-lit">gay-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2462288694" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5645783104204901294">We interrupt this broadcast to present you this breaking news:<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1532503946ra/26025010.gif' width='400' height='200' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Your regularly scheduled programming will resume once the emotions of this reviewer have been properly boxed and tamed. <br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1532503946ra/26025011.gif' width='400' height='216' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><u>
  <b>Poems:</b>
</u><br/><br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[
  <b>Scheherazade</b>
  <br/>
  <br/>
  <i>Tell me about the dream where we pull the bodies out of the lake<br/>and dress them in warm clothes again.<br/>How it was late, and no one could sleep, the horses running<br/>until they forget that they are horses.<br/>It’s not like a tree where the roots have to end somewhere,<br/>it’s more l</i></span></span>
  <span id="freeText5645783104204901294" style="display:none">We interrupt this broadcast to present you this breaking news:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1532503946ra/26025010.gif" width="400" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Your regularly scheduled programming will resume once the emotions of this reviewer have been properly boxed and tamed. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1532503946ra/26025011.gif" width="400" height="216" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><u>
  <b>Poems:</b>
</u><br><br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <b>Scheherazade</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>Tell me about the dream where we pull the bodies out of the lake<br>and dress them in warm clothes again.<br>How it was late, and no one could sleep, the horses running<br>until they forget that they are horses.<br>It’s not like a tree where the roots have to end somewhere,<br>it’s more like a song on a policeman’s radio,<br>how we rolled up the carpet so we could dance, and the days<br>were bright red, and every time we kissed there was another apple<br>to slice into pieces.<br>Look at the light through the windowpane. That means it’s noon, that means<br>we’re inconsolable.<br>Tell me how all this, and love too, will ruin us.<br>These, our bodies, possessed by light.<br>Tell me we’ll never get used to it.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <b>Little Beast</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>3<br>History repeats itself. Somebody says this.<br>History throws its shadow over the beginning, over the desktop,<br>over the sock drawer with its socks, its hidden letters.<br>History is a little man in a brown suit<br>trying to define a room he is outside of.<br>I know history. There are many names in history<br>but none of them are ours.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>4<br>He had green eyes,<br>so I wanted to sleep with him—<br>green eyes flecked with yellow, dried leaves on the surface of a pool-<br>You could drown in those eyes, I said.<br>The fact of his pulse,<br>the way he pulled his body in, out of shyness or shame or a desire<br>not to disturb the air around him.<br>Everyone could see the way his muscles worked,<br>the way we look like animals,<br>his skin barely keeping him inside.<br>I wanted to take him home<br>and rough him up and get my hands inside him, drive my body into his<br>like a crash test car.<br>I wanted to be wanted and he was<br>very beautiful, kissed with his eyes closed, and only felt good while moving.<br>You could drown in those eyes, I said,<br>so it’s summer, so it’s suicide,<br>so we’re helpless in sleep and struggling at the bottom of the pool.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1532507183ra/26025241.gif" width="400" height="280" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>7<br>What would you like? I’d like my money’s worth.<br>Try explaining a life bundled with episodes of this—<br>swallowing mud, swallowing glass, the smell of blood<br>on the first four knuckles.<br>We pull our boots on with both hands<br>but we can’t punch ourselves awake and all I can do<br>is stand on the curb and say Sorry<br>about the blood in your mouth. I wish it was mine.<br>I couldn’t get the boy to kill me, but I wore his jacket for the longest time.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <b>The Torn-Up Road</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>4<br>I want to tell you this story without having to say that I ran out into the street<br>to prove something, that he chased after me<br>and threw me into the gravel.<br>And he knew it wasn’t going to be okay, and he told me<br>it wasn’t going to be okay.<br>And he wouldn’t kiss me, but he covered my body with his body<br>and held me down until I promised not to run back out into the street again.<br>But the minutes don’t stop. The prayer of going nowhere<br>going nowhere.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <b>A Primer for the Small Weird Loves</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>1<br>The blond boy in the red trunks is holding your head underwater<br>because he is trying to kill you,<br>and you deserve it, you do, and you know this,<br>and you are ready to die in this swimming pool<br>because you wanted to touch his hands and lips and this means<br>your life is over anyway.<br>You’re in the eighth grade. You know these things.<br>You know how to ride a dirt bike, and you know how to do<br>long division,<br>and you know that a boy who likes boys is a dead boy, unless<br>he keeps his mouth shut, which is what you<br>didn’t do,<br>because you are weak and hollow and it doesn’t matter anymore.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>7<br>The stranger says there are no more couches and he will have to<br>sleep in your bed. You try to warn him, you tell him<br>you will want to get inside him, and ruin him,<br>but he doesn't listen.<br>You do this, you do. You take the things you love<br>and tear them apart<br>or you pin them down with your body and pretend they're yours.<br>So, you kiss him, and he doesn't move, he doesn't<br>pull away, and you keep on kissing him. And he hasn't moved,<br>he's frozen, and you've kissed him, and he'll never forgive you, and maybe now he'll leave you alone.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <b>I Had a Dream About You</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>In the dream I don’t tell anyone, I’m afraid to wake you up.<br>In these dreams it’s always you:<br>The boy in the sweatshirt,<br>The boy on the bridge, the boy who always keeps me<br>from jumping off the bridge.<br>Oh, the things we invent when we are scared<br>and want to be rescued.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532506062i/26025161._SY540_.jpg" width="200" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  <br>
  <br>
  <b>Straw House, Straw Dog</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>2<br>Four dreams in a row, four dreams in a row, four dreams in a row,<br>fall down right there. I wanted to fall down right there but I knew<br>you wouldn’t catch me because you’re dead. I swallowed crushed ice<br>pretending it was glass and you’re dead. Ashes to ashes.<br><br>You wanted to be cremated so we cremated you and you wanted an adventure<br>so I ran and I knew you wouldn’t catch me.<br>You are a fever I am learning to live with, and everything is happening<br>at the wrong end of a very long tunnel.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <b>Saying Your Names</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>All night I stretched my arms across<br>him, rivers of blood, the dark woods, singing<br>with all my skin and bone Please keep him safe.<br>Let him lay his head on my chest and we will be<br>like sailors, swimming in the sound of it, dashed<br>to pieces. Makes a cathedral, him pressing against<br>me, his lips at my neck, and yes, I do believe<br>his mouth is heaven, his kisses falling over me<br>like stars.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <b>You Are Jeff</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>4<br>Your name is Jeff and somewhere up ahead of you your brother has<br>pulled to the side of the road and he is waiting for you with a lug wrench<br>clutched in his greasy fist. 0 how he loves you, darling boy. 0 how, like<br>always, he invents the monsters underneath the bed to get you to sleep<br>next to him, chest to chest or chest to back, the covers drawn around<br>you in an act of faith against the night. When he throws the wrench into<br>the air it will catch the light as it spins toward you. Look—it looks like<br>a star. You had expected something else, anything else, but the wrench<br>never reaches you. It hangs in the air like that, spinning in the air like<br>that. It’s beautiful.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>6<br>You are playing cards with three men named Jeff. Two of the Jeffs seem<br>somewhat familiar, but the Jeff across from you keeps staring at your<br>hands, your mouth, and you’re certain that you’ve never seen this Jeff<br>before. But he’s on your team, and you’re ahead, you’re winning big,<br>and yet the other Jeffs keep smiling at you like there’s no tomorrow.<br>They all have perfect teeth: white, square, clean, even. And, for some<br>reason, the lighting in the room makes their teeth seem closer than they<br>should be, as if each mouth was a place, a living room with pink carpet<br>and the window’s open. Come back from the window, Jefferson. Take off<br>those wet clothes and come over here, by the fire.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>15<br>Like sandpaper, the light, or a blessing, or a bruise. Blood everywhere,<br>he said, the red light hemorrhaging from everywhere at once. The train<br>station blue, your lips blue, hands cold and the blue wind. Or a horse,<br>your favorite horse now raised up again out of the mud and galloping<br>galloping always toward you. In your ruined shirt, on the last day, while<br>the bruise won’t heal, and the stain stays put, the red light streaming in<br>from everywhere at once. Your broken ribs, the back of your head, your<br>hand to mouth or hand to now, right now, like you mean it, like it’s split-<br>ting you in two. Now look at the lights, the lights.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>21<br>Hold onto your voice. Hold onto your breath. Don’t make a noise,<br>don’t leave the room until I come back from the dead for you. I will<br>come back from the dead for you. This could be a city. This could be a<br>graveyard. This could be the basket of a big balloon. Leave the lights<br>on. Leave a trail of letters like those little knots of bread we used to<br>dream about. We used to dream about them. We used to do a lot of<br>things. Put your hand to the knob, your mouth to the hand, pick up the<br>bread and devour it. I’m in the hallway again, I’m in the hallway. The<br>radio’s playing my favorite song. Leave the lights on. Keep talking. I’ll<br>keep walking toward the sound of your voice.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>24<br>You’re in a car with a beautiful boy, and he won’t tell you that he loves<br>you, but he loves you. And you feel like you’ve done something terr-<br>ible, like robbed a liquor store, or swallowed pills, or shoveled yourself<br>a grave in the dirt, and you’re tired. You’re in a car with a beautiful boy,<br>and you’re trying not to tell him that you love him, and you’re trying to<br>choke down the feeling, and you’re trembling, but he reaches over and<br>he touches you, like a prayer for which no words exist, and you feel your<br>heart taking root in your body, like you’ve discovered something you<br>don’t even have a name for.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <b>Snow and Dirty Rain</b>
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>I had a dream about you. We were in the gold room<br>where everyone finally gets what they want.<br>You said Tell me about your books, your visions made<br>of flesh and light and I said This is the Moon. This is<br>the Sun. Let me name the stars for you. Let me take you<br>there. The splash of my tongue melting you like a sugar<br>cube... We were in the gold room where everyone<br>finally gets what they want, so I said What do you<br>want, sweetheart? and you said Kiss me. Here I am<br>leaving you clues. I am singing now while Rome<br>burns. We are all just trying to be holy. My applejack,<br>my silent night, just mash your lips against me.<br>We are all going forward. None of us are going back.</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532506725i/26025201.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]> <span id="freeTextContainer13974021736610962178"><b>
  <i>Crush</i>
</b> was so damn bad. I hate so much to DNF a book and this only has 62 pages and I wanted so bad to DNF.<br/><br/>This little poetry book is divided into three parts, the author at first doesn't tell you what is going on but later all the parts are related and is kind of a story. Mostly every chapter contain the word “kill”, “suicide”, and “hit” it was tiresome reading the same chapters over and over with the same words just in different scenarios, the person who speak and tells these stories is in </span>
  <span id="freeText13974021736610962178" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>Crush</i>
</b> was so damn bad. I hate so much to DNF a book and this only has 62 pages and I wanted so bad to DNF.<br><br>This little poetry book is divided into three parts, the author at first doesn't tell you what is going on but later all the parts are related and is kind of a story. Mostly every chapter contain the word “kill”, “suicide”, and “hit” it was tiresome reading the same chapters over and over with the same words just in different scenarios, the person who speak and tells these stories is in an abusive relationship and want to escape so this person just create scenarios in their head and speak aloud for some kind of liberation. I only liked the first but after that every section describes how this abusive relationship started and grew.<br><br>I will just leave my favorite part of this poetry book.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1600477604i/30125189.jpg" width="500" height="325" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_3003555" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3003555" />
    <a title="Patrick Duggan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/188161-patrick-duggan"><img alt="Patrick Duggan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1323042764p2/188161.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3003555?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick Duggan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick Duggan" href="/user/show/188161-patrick-duggan">Patrick Duggan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/188161-patrick-duggan?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3003555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6395365085943521039">SIken's <i>Crush</i>, his first book which also won the Yale Young Poets' award in 2004, is one of he most complete works of poetry I've come across in years. <br/><br/>He uses the pacing of his long line to slow time, and create a darker atmosphere within the verse, where shadows move from walls and creep along the legs of lovers. Time drags in elongated moments, or appears in flashes of memory and scenescape. His pace and image teach us fight from the first two pages how to read the work, and how to prepare yo</span>
  <span id="freeText6395365085943521039" style="display:none">SIken's <i>Crush</i>, his first book which also won the Yale Young Poets' award in 2004, is one of he most complete works of poetry I've come across in years. <br /><br />He uses the pacing of his long line to slow time, and create a darker atmosphere within the verse, where shadows move from walls and creep along the legs of lovers. Time drags in elongated moments, or appears in flashes of memory and scenescape. His pace and image teach us fight from the first two pages how to read the work, and how to prepare yourself for the worlds of panic, death, and love which are to come. Siken reminisces in sadness and joy, madness and damagingly clear thought. He pairs image and notion with time and yearning. There is beauty in the voice and damage of this book.<br /><br />Siken's poems are punk rock anthems, old country ballads, 60's B-movies, pulp novels, tin pail lunch boxes stuffed with old polaroids and love letters. His poems progress to a down tempo drum beat, and the skill in line break leaves the reader constantly moving forward, the combination forces us to digest and contemplate the words as they come, but never let up a moment for us to stop chewing. It's almost dumbfounding how Siken combines the long breath of a Ginsberg with the complete, unornamental word choice of a Creeley. <br /><br /><i>Crush</i> is a project in obsession. The repetition of pacing and break builds on the down tempo into a culminating panic under the weight of body and the gravity of obsessive love. Siken has, within <i>Crush</i>, created a world of love and death, of paranoia, where voices drift in and out, where the self questions its other aloud, causing disbelief in the fact of the world even as it builds around us into existence.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6395365085943521039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer563758628" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11502324387855238524">Once again, I return to rating poetry on a scale of "how much of it did I understand?" This one's language is easy to follow and the entire thing is comprehensive and you can really see the emotions and angst, but still, I couldn't find any deeper meanings in the poems. Perhaps I couldn't relate to them, but for the majority of this, I wasn't impressed.<br/><br/>My favorites from this collection: Little Beast, Litany in which Certain Things are Crossed Out, and You Are Jeff #24</span>

<div id="review_567705531" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/567705531" />
    <a title="Daniel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3977748-daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1418943509p2/3977748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/567705531?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel" href="/user/show/3977748-daniel">Daniel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3977748-daniel?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3977748-daniel?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3977748-daniel?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer567705531" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7670225084628510813">Do you remember Prometheus?<br/><br/>That thief of fire who was bound to the rock in order for the vulture to pick at his liver, every day? That liver grew back every day for the sole purpose of being eaten again. The liver wasn’t helpful to Prometheus, it was only a source of pain. Can you imagine what it would be like to know that your liver would be eaten from your body day after day? There must be an immense sense of dread, knowing that what’s going to happen today is inevitable. <br/><br/>This is not a book ab</span>
  <span id="freeText7670225084628510813" style="display:none">Do you remember Prometheus?<br /><br />That thief of fire who was bound to the rock in order for the vulture to pick at his liver, every day? That liver grew back every day for the sole purpose of being eaten again. The liver wasn’t helpful to Prometheus, it was only a source of pain. Can you imagine what it would be like to know that your liver would be eaten from your body day after day? There must be an immense sense of dread, knowing that what’s going to happen today is inevitable. <br /><br />This is not a book about Prometheus, but it may as well be. (We are playing with fire here, after all. At least, love can feel like a fire.) Every poem in this book is essentially the same. The poems are strong individually, but read together, they build something stronger. Images are repeated again and again with only slight variations (driving on the road, running out onto the road, lying in the road). The poems can’t help but to return to the same thing again. It’s painful, but it’s a delicious pain, glorious in love and lust and in being alternately strong and vulnerable. <br /><br />What the book doesn’t tell you directly is that Richard Siken was partially influenced by the death of his boyfriend. I don’t want to make any assumptions here about how that has influenced the content, but I will say that the poems read like a lover trying to move on from something that is, well, crushing. Moving on is not something you can just will yourself to do. <br /><br />In the end, the speaker believes he has escaped the cycle: “We are all going forward. None of us are going back.” Maybe, maybe not. It may be that moving forward requires a lot of going round in circles. And we know where those circles are going to take us. <br /><br />“We’ve been driving all night.<br />				We’ve been driving a long time.<br />	We want to stop. We can’t.”<br /><br />But why would we want to stop?</span>
  <a data-text-id="7670225084628510813" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14208967733595797362">I think that's the most beautiful piece of poetry I've ever read. I won't convince you. Here's my fav poem.<br/><br/><b>Little Beast</b><br/><br/>1<br/>An all-night barbecue. A dance on the courthouse lawn.<br/> The radio aches a little tune thet tells the story of what the night <br/>is thinking. It's thinking of love.<br/> It's thinking about stabbing us to death <br/>and leaving our bodies in a dumpster.<br/> That's a nice touch, stains in the night, whiskey kisses for everyone.<br/><br/><br/>Tonight, by the freeway, a man eating a fruit pie with a buckknife<br/> carv</span>
  <span id="freeText14208967733595797362" style="display:none">I think that's the most beautiful piece of poetry I've ever read. I won't convince you. Here's my fav poem.<br /><br /><b>Little Beast</b><br /><br />1<br />An all-night barbecue. A dance on the courthouse lawn.<br /> The radio aches a little tune thet tells the story of what the night <br />is thinking. It's thinking of love.<br /> It's thinking about stabbing us to death <br />and leaving our bodies in a dumpster.<br /> That's a nice touch, stains in the night, whiskey kisses for everyone.<br /><br /><br />Tonight, by the freeway, a man eating a fruit pie with a buckknife<br /> carves the likeness of his lover's face into the motel wall. I like him<br />and I want to be like him, my hands no longer an afterthought.<br /><br />2 <br />Someone once told me that explaining is an admission of failure.<br /> I'm sure you remember. I was on the phone with you, sweetheart.<br /><br />3<br />History repeats itself. Somebody says this.<br /> History throws its shadow over the beginning, over the desktop,<br />over the sock drawer with its socks, its hidden letters.<br /> History is a little man in a brown suit <br /> trying to define a room he is outside of.<br />I know history. There are many names in the history <br /> but none of them are ours.<br /><br />4<br /><br />He had green eyes,<br /> so I wanted to sleep with him<br /> green eyes flicked with yellow, dried leaves on the surface of a pool--<br />You could drown in those eyes,I said.<br /> The fact of his pulse,<br />the way he pulled his body in, out of shyness or shame or a desire<br /> not to disturb the air around him.<br />Everyone could see the way his muscles worked,<br /> the way we look like animals,<br /> his skin barely keeping him inside.<br /> I wanted to take him home<br />and rough him up and get my hands inside him, drive my body into his<br /> like a crash test car.<br /> I wanted to be wanted and he was<br />very beautiful, kissed with his eyes closed, and only felt good when moving.<br /> You could drown in those eyes, I said,<br /> so it's summer, so it's suicide,<br />so we're helpless in sleep and struggling at the bottom of the pool.<br /><br />5<br />It wasn't until we were well past the middle of it<br /> that we realized<br />the old dull pain, whose stitched wrist and clammy fingers,<br /> far from being subverted,<br />had only slipped underneath us, freshly scrubbed.<br /> Mirrors and shop windows returned our faces to us,<br /> replete with tight lips and the eyes that remained eyes<br /> and not the doorway we had hoped for.<br />His wounds healed, the skin a bit thicker that before,<br /> scars like train tracks on his arms and on his body underneath his shirt.<br /><br />6<br />We still groped for each other on the backstairs or in parked cars<br /> as the road around us<br />grew glossy with ice and our breath softened the view trough the glass<br /> already laced with frost,<br />but more frequently I was finding myself sleepless, and he was running out of<br /> lullabies.<br />But damn if there isn't anything sexier<br /> than a slender boy with a handgun.<br /> a fast car, a bottle of pills.<br /><br />7<br />What would you like? I'd like my money's worth.<br /> Try explaining a life bundled with episodes of this--<br /> swallowing mud, swallowing glass, the smell of blood <br />on the first four knuckles.<br /> We pull our boots with both hands<br />but we can't punch ourselves awake and all I can do<br /> is stand on the curb and say <i>Sorry <br /> about the blood in your mouth, I wish it was mine.</i><br /><br />I couldn't get the boy to kill me, but I wore his jacket for the longest time.<br /><br /><br /><br />-------------------<br />I'm speechless.<br /> <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=3-star">3-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3150996654" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17213318328815298451">A poetry collection inconsolable of its particular homosexual aching and desire, <i>Crush</i> grinds words into a cup of caffeine-infused affection. Whilst it also traverses realisations and remembrances throughout the complications of same sex attraction, it is insatiably hungry for love and the many faces it dons. It ravages it with kisses until it's bruised. It leaves. It comes back. And there is a cyclic element amongst the relationship/s this collection macerates into nimble physical proclamations</span>
  <span id="freeText17213318328815298451" style="display:none">A poetry collection inconsolable of its particular homosexual aching and desire, <i>Crush</i> grinds words into a cup of caffeine-infused affection. Whilst it also traverses realisations and remembrances throughout the complications of same sex attraction, it is insatiably hungry for love and the many faces it dons. It ravages it with kisses until it's bruised. It leaves. It comes back. And there is a cyclic element amongst the relationship/s this collection macerates into nimble physical proclamations. At times sweet, on other times bitter, much bland on others and unfortunately never a perfect cup, there is an interesting touch of tenderness in the corners of its coarseness. But similar to any caffeinated drink, if taken in excess, <i>Crush</i> palpitates into a ramble of overlapping emotions, eroticism, and words with only nearly achieving any kind of infectious poetic vibrancy it probably aims for. It is fairly lovely but can feel overtly long-winded and repetitive. Two poems are added to my favourites nevertheless: <a href="https://amarantae.wordpress.com/2012/01/13/visible-world-richard-siken/" rel="nofollow noopener">Visible World</a> and <a href="https://ir.uiowa.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=5305&amp;context=iowareview" rel="nofollow noopener">I Had A Dream About You</a>.<br /><br />Excerpts from some poems I really liked:<br />"Tell me how all this, and love too, will ruin us.<br />These, our bodies, possessed by light.<br />Tell me we’ll never get used to it."<br />— from <b>Scheherazade</b><br /><br />"History repeats itself. Somebody says this.<br />History throws its shadow over the beginning, over the desktop,<br />over the sock drawer with its socks, its hidden letters.<br />History is a little man in a brown suit<br />trying to define a room he is outside of.<br />I know history. There are many names in history<br />but none of them are ours."<br />— from <b>Little Beast</b><br /><br />"You wanted happiness, I can’t blame you for that,<br />and maybe a mouth sounds idiotic when it blathers on about joy<br />but tell me<br />you love this, tell me you’re not miserable."<br />— from <b>Seaside Improvisation</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="17213318328815298451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1255820198" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1255820198" />
    <a title="mwpm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13827810-mwpm"><img alt="mwpm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516943741p2/13827810.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1255820198?book_show_action=true">Apr 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mwpm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mwpm" href="/user/show/13827810-mwpm">mwpm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to mwpm by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/14913776-chaneli">Chaneli</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13827810-mwpm?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1255820198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer131108026716706596"><blockquote>
  <i>The Yale Younger Poets prize is the oldest annual literary award in the United States. The competition is open to any American under forty years of age who has not previously published a volume of poetry.<br/><br/>The Thom Gunn Award is an annual literary award, presented by Publishing Triangle to honour works of gay male poetry. First presented in 2001 as the Triangle Award for Gay Poetry, the award was renamed in memory of American poet Thom Gunn, the award's first winner, following his death in 2004</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText131108026716706596" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>The Yale Younger Poets prize is the oldest annual literary award in the United States. The competition is open to any American under forty years of age who has not previously published a volume of poetry.<br /><br />The Thom Gunn Award is an annual literary award, presented by Publishing Triangle to honour works of gay male poetry. First presented in 2001 as the Triangle Award for Gay Poetry, the award was renamed in memory of American poet Thom Gunn, the award's first winner, following his death in 2004.</i>
</blockquote><br />Having won the Yale Younger Poets prize, CRUSH was published as part of the Yale Series of Younger Poets. What's interesting about the prize is the poet who made the selection, Louise Glück. Like Glück, Silken is evidently influenced by the confessional poets. In fact, Glück compares Silken, in the foreword, to another confessional poet: "Books of this kind dream big; they trust not only what drives them but the importance of what drives them. When they work, as Plath's <i>Ariel</i> works, they are unforgettable; they restore to poetry that sense of crucial moment and crucial utterance which may indeed be the great genius of the form." (pg. x) <br /><br />This may be the extent of the comparison between Glück and Siken. As Glück suggests, Siken - although deriving his inspiration, as she does, from personal life, giving the poems their confessional edge - is writing from a state of "panic". Or, more accurately, his writing captures the state of panic (whether or not the author himself is writing in a panic). Glück's begins her foreword: "This is a book about panic." (pg. vii) Blunt though it may be, the statement is accurate and pertinent. But to say that he is "the animal in the headlights" is not enough. For that reason Glück elaborates: "he is the animal in the headlights, paralyzed; he is also the speeding vehicle, the car that doesn't stop, the mechanism of flight." (pg. vii)<br /><br />Moreover, Glück and Siken are set apart by the generational gap. Indeed, Siken's poetry is rooted in the present. The panic that permeates his poetry may also be interpreted as the impatience that is characteristic of the internet generation. Indeed, technology has its place in Siken's poetry, as Glück observes: "Cameras are everywhere, and tapes, the means by which by which an instant can be replayed over and over, manipulated. The poems' tense playbacks and freeze frames - their strategies of control - delineate chilling certainties and immutabilities." (pg. viii)<br /><blockquote><blockquote><blockquote>Imagine this:</blockquote></blockquote><br />You're driving.<br /><blockquote>The sky's bright. You look great.<br /><blockquote>In a word, in a phrase, it's a movie,<br /><blockquote><blockquote>you're the star</blockquote></blockquote></blockquote><br />so smile for the camera, it's your big scene,<br /><blockquote>you know your lines.</blockquote><br /> - <b>Planet of Love</b> (pg. 39)</blockquote></blockquote><br /><br />The poet's use of imagery is remarkable. What's interesting is his emphasis on the body, specifically hands, which find their way into almost every (or every other) poem in the collection... <br /><blockquote>Wearing your clothes or standing in the shower for over an hour, pretending<br /><blockquote>that this skin is your skin, these hands your hands,<br /><blockquote><blockquote>these shins, these soapy flanks.</blockquote></blockquote><br /> - <b>Dirty Valentine</b> (pg. 4)</blockquote><br />*<br /><br />I take off my hands and I give them to you but you don't<br /><blockquote><blockquote><blockquote>want them, so I take them back</blockquote><br />and put them on the wrong way, the wrong wrists....</blockquote><br /> - <b>Seaside Improvisation</b> (pg. 8)</blockquote><br />*<br /><br />A man takes his sadness down to the river and throws it in the river<br /><blockquote>but then he's still left</blockquote><br />with the river. A man takes his sadness and throws it away<br /><blockquote><blockquote>but then he's still left with his hands.</blockquote><br /> - <b>Boot Theory</b> (pg. 21)</blockquote><br />*<br /><br />His hands keep turning into<br />birds, and his hands keep flying away<br />from him. <i>Eventually the birds must land</i>.<br /><blockquote> - <b>Unfinished Duet</b> (pg. 27)</blockquote></blockquote><br /><br />Contrary to the "confessional poet" label, the poet has a proclivity for othering himself, or othering the narrator of the poems who, the act would suggest, is someone other than Siken. Moreover, the narrator, who is someone other than Siken, is someone other than the narrator, thereby creating anonymity with ambiguity...<br /><blockquote><blockquote>I want to tell you this story without having to confess anything,</blockquote><br />without having to say that I ran out into the street to prove something,<br /><blockquote><blockquote><blockquote>that he didn't love me,</blockquote></blockquote></blockquote><br />that I wanted to be thrown over, possessed.<br /><blockquote><blockquote>I want to tell you this story without having to be in it:<br />Max in the wrong clothes. Max at the party, drunk again.</blockquote></blockquote><br />Max in the kitchen, in refrigerator light, his hand around the neck of a beer.<br /><blockquote> - <b>The Torn-Up Road</b> (pg. 9)</blockquote><br />*<br /><br />At first there were too many branches<br />so he cut them and then it was winter.<br /><i>He meaning you</i>....<br /><blockquote> - <b>Unfinished Duet</b> (pg. 26)</blockquote><br /><br />Contrary to the "confessional poet" label, the poet adopts poetic personae, names other than his own, to create a distance from the personal, from the confessional, as in the case of "Max" (in <i>The Torn-Up Road</i>); or writes in the second person, attributing the name to the reader, as in the case of "<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/150236.The_Dream_Songs" rel="nofollow noopener">Henry</a>" (in <i>The Dislocated Room</i> and <i>Wishbone</i>); or a narrative driven account of a person, who may be many people, named "Jeff" (in <i>You Are Jeff</i>)...<br /><blockquote><blockquote><blockquote><i>Henry</i>, he's saying. <i>Who is it that's talking?</i></blockquote></blockquote><br />I thought I heard the clink<br /><blockquote><blockquote>of ice to teeth. I thought I heard the clink of teeth to glass.</blockquote><br /><blockquote> - <b>The Dislocated Room</b> (pg. 47)</blockquote><br />*<br /><br />I know you want me to say it, Henry, it's in the script, you want me to say<br /><i>Lie doen on the bed, you're all I ever wanted and worth dying for, too...</i><br /><blockquote><blockquote>but I think I'd rather keep the bullet.</blockquote><br /> - Wishbone (pg. 41)</blockquote><br />*<br /><br />This is how you make the meaning, you take two things and try to define the space between them. Jeff or Jeff? Who do you want to be?<br /> - You Are Jeff, 18 (pg. 56)</blockquote><br />(Significantly, Siken addresses the names of things and the naming of things and the recollection of named things in <i>Saying Your Name</i>.)<br /><br />One poem in particular, <i>Straw House, Straw Dog</i>, with its use of repetition and, more significantly, its derangement of the repeated phrases, elicits comparison to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/213910.Louis_Zukofsky" title="Louis Zukofsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Louis Zukofsky</a>'s <i>Julia's Wild</i>...<br /><blockquote><pre>I watched TV. I had a Coke at the bar. I had four dreams in a row<br />where you were burned, about to burn, or still on fire.<br /> I watched TV. I had a Coke at the bar. I had four Cokes,<br />four dreams in a row.<br /><br />Here you are in the straw house, feeding the straw dog. Here you are<br /> in the wrong house, feeding the wrong dog. I had a Coke with ice.<br />I had four dreams on TV. You have a cold cold smile.<br /> You were burned, you were about to burn, you’re still on fire.<br /><br />Here you are in the straw house, feeding ice to the dog, and you wanted<br />an adventure, so I said <i>Have an adventure.</i><br />The straw about to burn, the straw on fire. Here you are on the TV,<br /> saying <i>Watch me, just watch me.</i><br /><blockquote> - <b>Straw House, Straw Dog</b> (pg. 31)</blockquote></pre><br />*<br /><br />Come shadow, come, and take this shadow up,<br />Come shadow shadow, come and take this up,<br />Come, shadow, come, and take this shadow up,<br />Come, come shadow, and take this shadow up,<br />Come, come and shadow, take this shadow up,<br />Come, up, come shadow and take this shadow,<br />And up, come, take shadow, come this shadow,<br />And up, come, come shadow, take this shadow,<br />And come shadow, come up, take this shadow,<br />Come up, come shadow this, and take shadow,<br />Up, shadow this, come and take shadow, come<br />Shadow this, take and come up shadow, come<br />Take and come, shadow, come up, shadow this,<br />Up, come and take shadow, come this shadow,<br />Come up, take shadow, and come this shadow,<br />Come and take shadow, come up this shadow,<br />Shadow, shadow come, come and take this up,<br />Come, shadow, take, and come this shadow, up,<br />Come shadow, come, and take this shadow up,<br />Come, shadow, come, and take this shadow up.<br /><blockquote> - Louis Zukofsky, "Julia's Wild" </blockquote></blockquote></blockquote></blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="131108026716706596" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1192914423" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1192914423" />
    <a title="Liz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14811808-liz"><img alt="Liz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585394064p2/14811808.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1192914423?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz" href="/user/show/14811808-liz">Liz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14811808-liz?shelf=poetry-of-every-kind">poetry-of-every-kind</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14811808-liz?shelf=worth-re-reading">worth-re-reading</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1192914423" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7628320051318037003">First, I found this fragment from <i>"Planet of Love"</i> on the internet: <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1423761077i/13668542.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><br/>I didn't pay much attention to the feelings it invoked the first time. I remember saving it amongst my favourites and then going back to business, however things changed when I found myself coming back to this exact fragment over and over and over again in the following days. <br/>What did I do afterwards? Right, I searched for some more fragments. And then I just ordered the whole book hoping that unlike most modern poetry collecti</span>
  <span id="freeText7628320051318037003" style="display:none">First, I found this fragment from <i>"Planet of Love"</i> on the internet: <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1423761077i/13668542.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>I didn't pay much attention to the feelings it invoked the first time. I remember saving it amongst my favourites and then going back to business, however things changed when I found myself coming back to this exact fragment over and over and over again in the following days. <br>What did I do afterwards? Right, I searched for some more fragments. And then I just ordered the whole book hoping that unlike most modern poetry collections it would not disappoint me. <br>It didn't. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1423761854i/13668726.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>This was a piece of art. A dark, partially even morbid and bloodthirsty atmosphere, language that was on the edge between hatred and love, and the feeling of pain and pleasure. More pain though, yes, there was a lot of pain in this one, at least it seemed so to me. <br>And apparently I am very much drawn to such kind of suffering of the narrator/protagonist. Language was a tool in this collection, one that was skillfully wielded by somebody who knew what he wanted to convey. It was wonderful. <br>Sometimes confusing, sometimes heart-breaking, sometimes moving, but always wonderful. <br>The story, and there definitely was a plot-line, was lurking in the shadows, hiding in the darkness unless one looked at it from the corner of the eye instead of directly. That's when it showed itself, to be felt, to be understood, to be... <br>I don't think I am able to write a normal review for this one, it resonates too deep within me. Maybe because I have always tried to understand what this thing called love is and why it does to people what it does, maybe because I am slowly beginning to find bits and pieces of the answer, who knows?! <br>Fact is, I well and truly loved this collection and I am sure I will be coming back to it over and over again. Besides, it is Glbt, and I have grown rather tired of the usual and by now unfortunately stereotyped pairings. <br><br>Highly recommended for those who love or like poetry, for those who are curious about it, for those who enjoy Glbt, for those who want something complex and simple at the same time and for those who like to try out reading something new. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1423762672i/13668850.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="7628320051318037003" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9589869175736589534"><i> Is that too much to expect? That I would name the stars for you? </i><br/><br/>There were some parts here and there that caught my interest, but other than that this poetry collection kind of disappointed me. I expected a lot more and instead I found myself bored and confused on multiple occasions. This just wasn't the read for me.</span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1704186252?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="⊱ Poppy ⊰" class="user" itemprop="url" name="⊱ Poppy ⊰" href="/user/show/47555322-poppy">⊱ Poppy ⊰</a>
      </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1704186252" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer18067501555604074484">Well, HONESTLY speaking I started this book with really high hope's. Richard Siken is pretty much an spectacular writer, but this book was JUST not for me! The style and writing was awesome, if I was a critic I would've rated this book a <b>5star</b>, but NO I am NOT a critic and I will only give my precious stars to the book I love!!<br/><br/><blockquote>

  <span id="freeText18067501555604074484" style="display:none">Well, HONESTLY speaking I started this book with really high hope's. Richard Siken is pretty much an spectacular writer, but this book was JUST not for me! The style and writing was awesome, if I was a critic I would've rated this book a <b>5star</b>, but NO I am NOT a critic and I will only give my precious stars to the book I love!!<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"Tell me how all this, and love too, will ruin us.<br />These, our bodies, possessed by light. <br />Tell me we'll never get used to it."</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />But some poetry hit home!! Like literally it touched my heart!! The start was so good that I thought <u>WOW IT'S GONNA BE A MASTERPIECE♥.</u> But I was wrong, I don't know why was this hype about. This book was all about <b>blood, stitches, roads, dreams.</b> and some of the poems was so confusing, I didn't even get what exactly the poet was trying to convey. I just couldn't relate my self with the narrator. It was really annoying how his moods were shifting through every verse. At one point it was hard getting from one page to another. Although, this book was pretty much short.<br />I liked some of poetry which made me want to finish this book. So, I did and here we are giving this book a <b>2 Star</b>, JUST because I liked some poetry, and DAMN what a poetry that was! Made me incapable of escaping this book.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Tell me how all this, and love too, will ruin us.<br />These, our bodies, possessed by light.<br />Tell me we'll never get used to it.” </i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />These Lines really hit home. It was so beautiful that for a second I thought DAMN man, it's something else!! ♥♥<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i><br />“Tell me about the dream where we pull the bodies out of the lake<br />                                                                                and dress them in warm clothes again.<br />          How it was late, and no one could sleep, the horses running<br />until they forget that they are horses.<br />                    It’s not like a tree where the roots have to end somewhere,<br />          it’s more like a song on a policeman’s radio,<br />                              how we rolled up the carpet so we could dance, and the days<br />were bright red, and every time we kissed there was another apple<br />                                                                                                                        to slice into pieces.<br />Look at the light through the windowpane. That means it’s noon, that means<br />          we're inconsolable.<br />                                                            Tell me how all this, and love too, will ruin us.<br />These, our bodies, possessed by light.<br />                                                                                          Tell me we’ll never get used to it.” </i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />How can love ruin us??♥ It can't <b>Love</b> make us see light and sometimes the fucking stars, it make us see this world in new light and make us think every thought twice. We can literally see before and after love that how much it changed us.♥ and i loved how author point out the real meaning behind it. These were the moment I LOVED this book very much. I hoped that every line would've been THIS great for me!! But unfortunately this was NOT for me.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“A man takes his sadness down to the river and throws it in the river<br />                    but then he’s still left<br />with the river. A man takes his sadness and throws it away<br />                                                                        but then he’s still left with his hands.” </i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br /><b>2 STARS for this beautiful lines♥!!</b> JUST because of these, nothing more, nothing else.</span>

<div id="review_12648291" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12648291" />
    <a title="tee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/789575-tee"><img alt="tee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1389318091p2/789575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12648291?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="tee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="tee" href="/user/show/789575-tee">tee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/789575-tee?shelf=i-own">i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/789575-tee?shelf=poets">poets</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12648291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13544175506563738016">I've had this book on my shelf for ages and have been holding off reading it because I didn't want to read it and it be over. You know how it goes? So I kept saving it. My partner pointed it out to me the other day (I keep it on my bedside table), and said, "uh-oh, she's drawn all over the cover." - referring to my 1.5 year old daughter. Seeing my crestfallen face he asked what book it was and I said, "Oh, just only my favourite." Even though I hadn't yet read it. I had breathed in snatches of i</span>
  <span id="freeText13544175506563738016" style="display:none">I've had this book on my shelf for ages and have been holding off reading it because I didn't want to read it and it be over. You know how it goes? So I kept saving it. My partner pointed it out to me the other day (I keep it on my bedside table), and said, "uh-oh, she's drawn all over the cover." - referring to my 1.5 year old daughter. Seeing my crestfallen face he asked what book it was and I said, "Oh, just only my favourite." Even though I hadn't yet read it. I had breathed in snatches of it on the sly, a sentence here and there and it seemed like it was going to be devastatingly good. <br /><br />I like the way Siken lays his words out on the page and I love the words themselves. There's sadness, self-pity and a tremor of anxiety. Love and obsession and confusion. But sometimes I felt like I didn't understand what he was writing so beautifully about, as though it was a letter to someone in the know and I was reading with prying eyes.I'd love to understand more fully what some of it means, poetry like this tends to go right over my head. When I first started reading Anne Sexton's work, I felt the same way - but upon reading several biographies I started to understand her and her writing. I'd love to go over this book with a Smart Poetry Person and a fine tooth comb, or climb inside Siken's head and see the workings of his mind. So me being a poetry simpleton is no fault of Siken's, but I can't give this a five star if I don't fully comprehend the content. Still beautiful, and I'm sure I'm going to read it over and over and over. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13544175506563738016" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1990505690" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1990505690" />
    <a title="Pavellit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54586399-pavellit"><img alt="Pavellit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482125863p2/54586399.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1990505690?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pavellit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pavellit" href="/user/show/54586399-pavellit">Pavellit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">If you feel like you've spent too much time doing crap things.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54586399-pavellit?shelf=glbt">glbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54586399-pavellit?shelf=short">short</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54586399-pavellit?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1990505690" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3242660028809537933">I have always wondered what is the essence that makes prose a lasting memory and deeply flows into my veins. Reading this collection of poems allowed me to see it clearly. The poetic bits are the thing ingrained (encoded) in my sensitivity system. <br/><br/>This work is more than a fascinating compilation of language. I've seen the intricacies and strings of thought that carry through each section. It truly reached in deep and took me along with it. <br/><br/><i>'Tell me about the dream where we pull the bodies out of</i></span>
  <span id="freeText3242660028809537933" style="display:none">I have always wondered what is the essence that makes prose a lasting memory and deeply flows into my veins. Reading this collection of poems allowed me to see it clearly. The poetic bits are the thing ingrained (encoded) in my sensitivity system. <br /><br />This work is more than a fascinating compilation of language. I've seen the intricacies and strings of thought that carry through each section. It truly reached in deep and took me along with it. <br /><br /><i>'Tell me about the dream where we pull the bodies out of the lake and dress them in warm clothes again.'</i> begins the first poem and the rest proceeds like a dream that is also a nightmare. Seemed like specific moments. Full of moments that are injected with lust, fear, desire, obsession, panic, and anxiety.<br /><br />People who claim not to like poetry, after reading a single poem from Richard Siken’s Crush (I hope so) will change poetry for them, because it is something that is passionate, tender, and complicated, but also so <i>
  <b>accessible</b>
</i>. </span>

          </span>
